What about n=5? Let's test if k=2 possible? Might be that the only possible k are 0,1,3,..., maybe up to n-2? But we need to determine all k for any n≥3.

We should try to find a general construction. The condition: For all positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1, point (a,b) lies on at least one line. So we need to cover a triangular grid of points. This is reminiscent of covering points with lines, where the lines can be chosen to be lines with equations of the form x = i, y = i, x+y = i. But those are not sunny. So to have sunny lines, we need to replace some of these with other slopes.

Observation: For n≥3, we can always take n-1 vertical lines x=1,...,n-1 and one sunny line through (n,1) (and maybe also through other points if needed). That gives k=1. But can we get k=2? Possibly by taking n-2 vertical lines and two sunny lines that together cover the points with x=n-1 and x=n. But as we saw, the points with x=n-1: (n-1,1) and (n-1,2) if n-1+2 ≤ n+1? Actually for x=n-1, b can be from 1 to (n+1)-(n-1)=2, so points (n-1,1) and (n-1,2). For x=n, only (n,1). So three points. To cover them with two sunny lines, we need a sunny line through two of them, but any two of these three determine a line that is either vertical (x=n-1) or horizontal (y=1) or slope -1 (if (n-1,2) and (n,1) slope -1). So no sunny line through two of them. So we cannot cover three points with two sunny lines if the only lines through two points are non-sunny. However, could we cover them with two sunny lines that each pass through only one of the points? But then we need to cover three points with two lines; if each line passes through only one point, then one point is not covered. So we need at least three lines to cover three points if each line can cover at most one point from that triple. But we have only two sunny lines, and we also have the non-sunny lines covering some of the other points. However, the points with x=n-1 and x=n are only covered by the sunny lines if the non-sunny lines don't cover them. In our construction, we are using vertical lines x=1,...,n-2 to cover points with x=1 to n-2. The points with x=n-1 and x=n are not covered by those vertical lines. So they must be covered by the remaining two lines (which are sunny in this case, since we want exactly 2 sunny lines). So we have 2 lines to cover 3 points. As argued, any line that covers two of these points must be non-sunny (vertical, horizontal, slope -1). So if we try to use a sunny line covering two points, that's impossible. So we would have to cover each point with a separate line; but we have only 2 lines for 3 points, impossible. Thus k=2 seems impossible if we use vertical lines to cover x=1..n-2 and then need to cover x=n-1 and x=n with 2 sunny lines. However, maybe we can use a different set of non-sunny lines, not just vertical. For instance, we could use non-sunny lines that cover more points, leaving fewer points to be covered by sunny lines. But we need exactly 2 sunny lines, so we have 2 non-sunny lines. They can cover up to how many points? Each non-sunny line can cover at most n points (if it is x=1 or y=1 or x+y=n+1? Actually for n, the line x=1 covers (1,1) to (1,n) - that's n points because a+b ≤ n+1, for x=1, b ≤ n, so there are n points. Similarly, y=1 covers n points. The line x+y=n+1 covers points with sum = n+1, which are (1,n), (2,n-1), ..., (n,1) - that's n points. So each non-sunny line can cover up to n points. Two such lines can cover at most 2n points if they are disjoint, but the total points are n(n+1)/2. For n≥3, n(n+1)/2 > 2n? Actually n(n+1)/2 - 2n = n(n+1-4)/2 = n(n-3)/2 ≥ 0. Thus for n=4, k=2 seems impossible. But we need to be thorough: Could there be a choice of two non-sunny lines that are not both of the types that cover many points, but maybe one non-sunny line covers only 1 point, leaving a large complement, but then we have 2 sunny lines that can cover up to 4 points; if the complement size is ≤4, we might still cover it with 2 sunny lines. So we need to consider all possible pairs of non-sunny lines, not just those that cover many points. The condition for k=2 is that we have 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines (total 4 lines). The non-sunny lines can be any lines that are not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1). They could be lines that cover very few points (e.g., y=4 covers only (1,4), x=4 covers only (4,1), x+y=2 covers only (1,1), etc.). If we choose such lines, the union may be small, leaving a large complement (≥5 points), which cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines (since each sunny line covers at most 2 points from the set, total 4). So the complement must be ≤4. So we need the union of the two non-sunny lines to cover at least 6 points. So we only need to consider pairs that cover at least 6 points. For n=4, the maximum union from two lines is 8 (if they are disjoint), but we need at least 6. So we need to consider all pairs that cover at least 6 points. Let's enumerate all possible pairs (L1, L2) where L1 and L2 are non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) and compute their union size and the complement. Since the set is small, we can brute force mentally. Let's list all non-sunny lines:

Horizontal: H1: y=1 (points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1)); H2: y=2 (points: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2)); H3: y=3 (points: (1,3),(2,3)); H4: y=4 (points: (1,4)).
Vertical: V1: x=1 (points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4)); V2: x=2 (points: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3)); V3: x=3 (points: (3,1),(3,2)); V4: x=4 (points: (4,1)).
Slope -1: D2: x+y=2 (points: (1,1)); D3: x+y=3 (points: (1,2),(2,1)); D4: x+y=4 (points: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)); D5: x+y=5 (points: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)).

Now, compute union sizes for each pair. We need union size ≥6. Since total points =10, complement size ≤4. Let's compute all pairs:

- V1 (4) and V2 (3): disjoint? V1 points: (1,1-4); V2 points: (2,1-3). They are disjoint because x=1 vs x=2. Union size = 4+3=7. Complement =3 points: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Already considered: no sunny pair.

- V1 and V3: V1 4, V3 2, disjoint? V1 x=1, V3 x=3, disjoint. Union=6, complement=4: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(4,1). We already considered that complement; sunny pair exists but we couldn't cover all 4 with 2 sunny lines because only one sunny pair and the other pair is not sunny. But could we cover the four points with two sunny lines where one line covers three points? No three collinear sunny. So we need to check if there is a covering of these four points by two sunny lines, not necessarily each line covering exactly two points from the set. For instance, maybe one sunny line covers (2,2) and (4,1) (sunny) and the other covers (2,1) and (2,3) but that line is vertical not sunny; but could the second line cover (2,1) alone and (2,3) alone? No, one line can only cover one of them if we cannot have a line through both that is sunny. So we need to cover all four points with two lines; each point must be on at least one line. So if we have a line L1 that goes through two points, the remaining points must be covered by L2. If L2 covers two points, it must go through them; but if the only possible pair from the remaining two is not sunny, then L2 cannot be sunny. So we need to see if there is a partition of the set into two pairs, each pair forming a sunny line. The only sunny pair among the four is (2,2)-(4,1). The remaining two are (2,1) and (2,3). The line through them is vertical, not sunny. So that partition fails. Could we have a different partition where the two pairs are not disjoint? For covering, we can have L1 cover one point, L2 cover the other three, but then L2 must cover three points; can it cover (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) with a sunny line? The line through (2,1) and (2,2) is vertical, not sunny; through (2,2) and (2,3) vertical; through (2,1) and (2,3) vertical; so no sunny line covers three of them because any line through two of them is vertical; but a line could be a different line that passes through only one of them and also covers the other two? Actually if L2 is a line, it can contain at most 2 of these points if they are collinear; but if it contains only one, then the other two points would need to be covered by L1? But we have only two lines. So we need to cover all points. Let's denote the four points: A=(2,1), B=(2,2), C=(2,3), D=(4,1). We need two lines L1, L2 such that each of A,B,C,D lies on at least one of them. Since L1 and L2 are lines, they are infinite. The points are not collinear in triples; check if any three are collinear? A,B,C are collinear vertical x=2; B,C,D: B(2,2), C(2,3), D(4,1) are not collinear (slope from B to D = (1-2)/(4-2) = -1/2, from C to D = (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1, not equal). So no three collinear. So each line can contain at most 2 points from the set. Thus we need to partition the four points into two pairs, each pair lying on a line (the line through the pair). So the only possible covering with two lines is that L1 is the line through a pair, L2 is the line through the other pair. So we need to find a partition of the set into two pairs such that both pairs are collinear (they always are) and the lines are sunny. So we need to check all possible pairings of the four points. The possible pairings: (A,B) and (C,D): lines: x=2 (vertical) and line through (2,3) and (4,1): slope -1, not sunny. (A,C) and (B,D): lines: x=2 (vertical) and line through (2,2) and (4,1): slope -1/2, sunny. So (A,C) gives vertical (non-sunny), (B,D) gives sunny. But L1 would be vertical (non-sunny) and L2 sunny; we need both lines sunny? Wait, we are allowed to have only 2 sunny lines total; the other 2 lines are non-sunny. In this scenario, we are using the two sunny lines to cover the complement. So both L1 and L2 must be sunny lines. So if we pair (A,C) and (B,D), then L1 is vertical (non-sunny) and L2 sunny; that's not allowed because we need both lines sunny. So that pairing fails. (A,D) and (B,C): lines: through (2,1) and (4,1) is horizontal y=1 (non-sunny), and through (2,2) and (2,3) is vertical x=2 (non-sunny). So both non-sunny. (A,B) and (C,D) we already did; (A,C) and (B,D) gave one sunny; (A,D) and (B,C) gave both non-sunny. So no partition gives both sunny. Therefore this complement cannot be covered by two sunny lines.

Thus V1 and V3 fails.

- V1 and V4: V1 4, V4 1, disjoint? V1 x=1, V4 x=4, disjoint. Union=5, complement=5 >4, so not possible.

- V2 (3) and V3 (2): disjoint? V2 x=2, V3 x=3, disjoint. Union=5, complement=5 >4.

- V2 and V4: disjoint? V2 x=2, V4 x=4, disjoint. Union=3+1=4, complement=6 >4.

- V3 and V4: disjoint? V3 x=3, V4 x=4, disjoint. Union=2+1=3, complement=7.

- H1 (4) and H2 (3): H1 y=1, H2 y=2, disjoint? y=1 and y=2, so no common points. Union=4+3=7, complement=3: (1,3),(2,3),(3,2)? Actually points not on H1 or H2: H1 covers (1,1)-(4,1); H2 covers (1,2),(2,2),(3,2). So left: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,1),(4,1)? Wait (3,1) is on H1? Actually (3,1) is on y=1, so covered. So remaining: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,1)? (3,1) covered by H1, so not; (4,1) covered by H1. So left: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2)?? Actually (3,2) is on H2, so covered. So left: (1,3),(1,4),(2,3) — that's 3 points. Check pairs: (1,3)-(1,4) vertical x=1; (1,3)-(2,3) horizontal y=3; (1,4)-(2,3) slope (3-4)/(2-1) = -1, not sunny. So no sunny pair. So fails.

- H1 and H3: H1 4, H3 2, disjoint? y=1 and y=3, disjoint. Union=6, complement=4: points not on y=1 or y=3: all points except (1,1)-(4,1) and (1,3),(2,3). So left: (1,2),(1,4),(2,2),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1) — that's 6 points? Actually compute: total 10 points; remove H1: 4 points; remove H3: 2 points; but are there overlaps? H1 and H3 are disjoint, so union size = 4+2=6, complement = 4 points. Which points? List all points: (1,1)-(1,4); (2,1)-(2,3); (3,1)-(3,2); (4,1). Remove y=1: remove (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1). Remove y=3: remove (1,3),(2,3). So left: (1,2),(1,4),(2,2),(3,2). That's 4 points. Check sunny pairs: (1,2)-(1,4) vertical; (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal; (1,2)-(3,2) horizontal; (1,4)-(2,2) slope (2-4)/(2-1) = -2, sunny; (1,4)-(3,2) slope (2-4)/(3-1) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny; (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal. So we have sunny pair (1,4)-(2,2) slope -2. Also (1,2)-(2,2) horizontal not; (1,2)-(3,2) horizontal; (1,4)-(3,2) slope -1; so only sunny pair is (1,4)-(2,2). Then we need to cover the four points with two sunny lines. One sunny line could be through (1,4) and (2,2). Then we need to cover (1,2) and (3,2) with the other sunny line. The line through (1,2) and (3,2) is horizontal y=2, not sunny. So fails.

- H1 and H4: H1 4, H4 1, disjoint, union=5, complement=5 >4.

- H2 and H3: H2 3, H3 2, disjoint? y=2 and y=3, disjoint. Union=5, complement=5.

- H2 and H4: H2 3, H4 1, disjoint, union=4, complement=6.

- H3 and H4: H3 2, H4 1, disjoint, union=3, complement=7.

- D2 (1) and D3 (2): D2: (1,1); D3: (1,2),(2,1). Intersection? D2 and D3 share? (1,1) is on D2; D3 has (1,2) and (2,1), so no common. Union = 1+2=3, complement=7 >4.

- D2 and D4: D2 (1,1); D4 (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Intersection? (1,1) is not in D4? D4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So no common. Union=4, complement=6.

- D2 and D5: D2 (1,1); D5 (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). No common. Union=5, complement=5.

- D3 and D4: D3 (1,2),(2,1); D4 (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Intersection? (2,1) vs D4? D4 has (3,1) and (2,2) and (1,3); no common. So union=4, complement=6.

- D3 and D5: D3 (1,2),(2,1); D5 (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Intersection? None. Union=6, complement=4: points not on D3 or D5: all points except (1,2),(2,1),(1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). So left: (1,1),(1,3),(2,2),(3,1). That's 4 points. Check sunny pairs among these: (1,1)-(1,3) vertical; (1,1)-(2,2) slope 1, sunny; (1,1)-(3,1) horizontal; (1,3)-(2,2) slope (2-3)/(2-1)=-1 not sunny; (1,3)-(3,1) slope (1-3)/(3-1)=-2/2=-1; (2,2)-(3,1) slope -1. So sunny pair: (1,1)-(2,2) slope 1. So we have a sunny pair (1,1)-(2,2). Then we need to cover (1,3) and (3,1) with the other sunny line. Check (1,3)-(3,1): slope (1-3)/(3-1)= -2/2=-1 not sunny; line through them is slope -1, not sunny. Could we cover them separately? No, one line left. So fails.

- D4 and D5: D4 (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); D5 (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Intersection? None? Check (2,2) vs D5? D5 has (2,3) and (3,2) and (1,4),(4,1); no common. So union=3+4=7, complement=3: points not on D4 or D5: all points except those 7. Which points? List all points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Remove D4: remove (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). Remove D5: remove (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Left: (1,1),(1,2),(2,1) — 3 points. Check pairs: (1,1)-(1,2) vertical; (1,1)-(2,1) horizontal; (1,2)-(2,1) slope -1. No sunny pair. So fails.

Thus for n=4, k=2 seems impossible. So far we have k=0,1,3 possible. What about k=2 for n=5? Let's test a potential construction. For n=5, points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). That's 15 points. We need 5 lines, exactly k sunny. For k=2, we need 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover many points; we need to cover all 15 points with 5 lines. The maximum coverage by a non-sunny line: x=1 covers 5 points; y=1 covers 5; x+y=6 covers 5 points (since a+b=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1)). So each non-sunny line can cover at most 5 points. With 3 non-sunny lines, we could cover up to 15 points if they are disjoint. But can we choose 3 non-sunny lines that together cover all 15 points? Possibly if they are x=1, y=1, x+y=6, and they are pairwise disjoint? Check: x=1 points: (1,1-5); y=1 points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1); they share (1,1). So not disjoint. Union of x=1 and y=1: 5+5-1=9 points; add x+y=6: points (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Some of these may already be covered: (1,5) is on x=1; (2,4) maybe not on x=1 or y=1? (2,4) not on x=1 (x=2) and not on y=1 (y=4). (3,3) not on x=1 or y=1? (3,3) not. (4,2) not. (5,1) is on y=1. So new points: (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) are new. Also (1,5) already, (5,1) already. So total union = 9+3=12 points. Still missing 3 points: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2)? Actually let's list all points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). Remove x=1: remove (1,1-5). Remove y=1: remove (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1),(5,1). So after removing both, left: (2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,2). That's 6 points. Then add x+y=6: remove (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Already removed (1,5) and (5,1) earlier; new: (2,4),(3,3),(4,2). So after adding, left: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2) — 3 points. So complement of these three lines is 3 points: (2,2),(2,3),(3,2). Check pairs: (2,2)-(2,3) vertical; (2,2)-(3,2) horizontal; (2,3)-(3,2) slope -1. No sunny pair. So we would need to cover these 3 points with 2 sunny lines. That's impossible as before. So that choice fails.

What if we choose different non-sunny lines? For n=5, we have many possible lines. Could we choose three non-sunny lines that cover all points except maybe a set that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? Since we need exactly 2 sunny lines, the complement must be coverable by 2 sunny lines. The complement points are those not on the non-sunny lines. The maximum size of the complement that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is 4 (if each sunny line covers 2 points from the complement, and no three collinear on a sunny line). So we need the union of the three non-sunny lines to cover at least 11 points. Since total 15, complement ≤4. So we need to find three non-sunny lines that together cover at least 11 points. Each non-sunny line can cover up to 5 points, so three lines can cover up to 15 if disjoint. But they may overlap. To cover at least 11 points, we need the union size ≥11. That is possible. Then the complement size ≤4. But we also need that the complement can be covered by 2 sunny lines. So we need to find if there exists a triple of non-sunny lines such that the uncovered points (≤4) contain a sunny pair (if 3 points) or can be partitioned into two sunny pairs (if 4 points). Since the set is larger, we can try to construct a configuration with 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines that works. Perhaps we can use the approach we used for n=4, k=3: we had n-1 vertical lines and one sunny line covering the points with x=n? That gave k=1. For k=2, maybe we can use n-2 vertical lines and two sunny lines covering the points with x=n-1 and x=n. But as argued, those points are three points (for n≥3) that cannot be covered by two sunny lines because any two of them determine a non-sunny line. However, maybe we can choose the non-sunny lines not to be vertical, but some other combination that leaves a complement that is coverable by two sunny lines. For n=5, the points with x=4 and x=5 are: (4,1),(4,2),(5,1) — that's 3 points. They are (4,1),(4,2),(5,1). As before, any two of these: (4,1)-(4,2) vertical; (4,1)-(5,1) horizontal; (4,2)-(5,1) slope -1. So no sunny pair. So if we use vertical lines x=1,2,3 (3 lines) to cover points with x=1..3, then we need to cover (4,1),(4,2),(5,1) with 2 sunny lines. That fails. So we need a different set of non-sunny lines. Perhaps we can use lines that are not all vertical; maybe we can use a line of slope -1 that covers some of these points, leaving a complement that is different. For example, take non-sunny lines: x=1 (4 points), x=2 (3 points), and x+y=6 (5 points). Their union might cover many points, leaving a small complement. Let's compute union of V1 (4), V2 (3), D5 (5). Points: V1: (1,1-5); V2: (2,1-4); D5: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Union: V1∪V2 = all points with x=1 or x=2: that's (1,1-5) and (2,1-4) = 5+4=9 points. Then add D5: new points from D5 not already covered: (3,3),(4,2),(5,1). (1,5) already in V1, (2,4) already in V2. So total union = 9+3=12 points. Complement = 3 points: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1) — these are (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Check pairs: (3,1)-(3,2) vertical; (3,1)-(4,1) horizontal; (3,2)-(4,1) slope -1. So no sunny pair. So fails.

Try V1, V2, D4 (x+y=4): D4 covers (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). V1∪V2 = 9 points; D4 new: (1,3) already in V1? Yes (1,3) in V1; (2,2) not in V1 or V2? V2 has (2,1)-(2,4) includes (2,2) so yes; (3,1) not in V1 or V2? V1 x=1, V2 x=2, so (3,1) is new. So union = 9+1=10 points. Complement =5 points: (1,4),(1,5),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1)? Actually we need to compute carefully. Let's list all points: 15 points. V1∪V2 = (1,1-5),(2,1-4). That's 5+4=9 points. D4 = (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So new points: (3,1) is new; (1,3) and (2,2) already covered. So union = 10 points. Complement = 5 points: (1,4),(1,5),(2,3),(2,4),(3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1) — wait that's 9 points? I'm messing. Let's list all 15 points systematically:

Points (a,b) with a,b≥1, a+b≤6:
a=1: b=1..5 -> (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5)
a=2: b=1..4 -> (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4)
a=3: b=1..3 -> (3,1),(3,2),(3,3)
a=4: b=1..2 -> (4,1),(4,2)
a=5: b=1 -> (5,1)
a=6: b=0? none, so total 5+4+3+2+1=15.

V1∪V2: x=1 points: (1,1)-(1,5); x=2 points: (2,1)-(2,4). So these are: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4),(1,5),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4). That's 9 points.

D4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). So (3,1) is new; (1,3) and (2,2) already covered. So union = 10 points: (1,1-5),(2,1-4),(3,1). Complement = 5 points: (3,2),(3,3),(4,1),(4,2),(5,1). That's 5 points. So complement size 5 >4. So not coverable by 2 sunny lines (max 4). So fails.

Try V1, D4, D5: V1 (5 points), D4 (3 points), D5 (5 points). Compute union: V1: (1,1-5). D4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1). D5: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Union: V1∪D4 = (1,1-5) and (2,2),(3,1) (since (1,3) already). So currently: (1,1-5),(2,2),(3,1). Then add D5: (1,5) already, (2,4) new, (3,3) new, (4,2) new, (5,1) new. So union = 5+2+4=11 points? Actually V1 5, D4 adds (2,2),(3,1) -> 2 more; D5 adds (2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) -> 4 more; total 5+2+4=11 points. Complement = 4 points: (2,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). That's (2,1),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1). Check sunny pairs: (2,1)-(2,3) vertical; (2,1)-(3,2) slope 1 sunny; (2,1)-(4,1) horizontal; (2,3)-(3,2) slope -1; (2,3)-(4,1) slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2=-1; (3,2)-(4,1) slope -1. So we have a sunny pair: (2,1)-(3,2) slope 1. Also (2,3)-(3,2) slope -1 not sunny; (2,1)-(2,3) vertical; (2,1)-(4,1) horizontal; (2,3)-(4,1) slope -1; (3,2)-(4,1) slope -1. So only sunny pair is (2,1)-(3,2). Then we need to cover the four points with two sunny lines. One sunny line could be through (2,1) and (3,2) (slope 1). Then we need to cover (2,3) and (4,1) with the other sunny line. The line through (2,3) and (4,1) has slope (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1, not sunny. Could we use a different sunny line for the first? Only one sunny pair exists. So that fails.

What about V2, D4, D5? V2: x=2 points: (2,1)-(2,4) (4 points); D4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); D5: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Union: V2 and D4: (2,1-4) and (1,3),(3,1) — (2,2) already in V2. So currently: (2,1-4),(1,3),(3,1). Add D5: (1,5) new, (2,4) already, (3,3) new, (4,2) new, (5,1) new. So union = 4+2+4=10 points? Actually V2 4, D4 adds (1,3),(3,1) -> 2 more; D5 adds (1,5),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1) -> 4 more; total 4+2+4=10. Complement = 5 points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,4),(2,? ) Actually points left: (1,1),(1,2),(1,4),(2,? ) (2,? ) V2 includes all x=2, so (2,1-4) covered; (3,2) is not covered? Let's list all points: (1,1-5); (2,1-4); (3,1-3); (4,1-2); (5,1). Covered: from V2: (2,1-4); from D4: (1,3),(3,1); from D5: (1,5),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). So covered points: (1,3),(1,5),(2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(2,4),(3,1),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). That's 10 points. Left: (1,1),(1,2),(1,4),(3,2),(4,1) — that's 5 points. So complement size 5, not good.

Try H1, D4, D5: H1: y=1 points: (1,1)-(5,1) (5 points); D4: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1); D5: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1). Union: H1∪D4: (1,1-5) and (2,2),(3,1) — (1,3) already. So currently: (1,1-5),(2,2),(3,1). Add D5: (1,5) already, (2,4) new, (3,3) new, (4,2) new, (5,1) already. So union = 5+2+3=10 points? Actually H1 5, D4 adds (2,2),(3,1) -> 2 more; D5 adds (2,4),(3,3),(4,2) -> 3 more; total 5+2+3=10. We need to find a general characterization. Let's think about the necessary condition. The condition that for all positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1, point (a,b) lies on at least one line. This is a covering problem. We have n lines. The points are a triangular grid. The lines can be any lines, not necessarily through two points. The restriction on sunny lines is that they cannot be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So they can have any other slope.

Observation: The set of points is symmetric under permutations of coordinates? Not exactly, but the condition a+b ≤ n+1 is symmetric in a and b? Actually if we swap a and b, the set is the same because a+b ≤ n+1 and a,b positive. So the set is symmetric under swapping coordinates. The lines we use are also symmetric in some sense.

Maybe we can think about the "lines" in the dual or something.

Another approach: Consider the integer lattice points. The lines are infinite. The condition that every point in the triangle lies on some line. This is reminiscent of covering a triangle with lines, which is a classic problem. For the set of points (i,j) with i,j positive and i+j ≤ n+1, the minimum number of lines needed to cover them is n (since we can use n vertical lines, or n horizontal lines, or n lines of slope -1). But can we cover them with fewer than n lines? For n=3, we need at least 3 lines? Actually we used 3 lines to cover 6 points; could we cover with 2 lines? So only two points. Another line could cover the rest? Possibly a line with slope -1 through (1,3),(2,2),(3,1) covers 3 points; but that's slope -1, not sunny. With 2 lines, we could cover all 6 points if one line covers 3 and the other covers the other 3. For n=3, can we find two lines that cover all 6 points? For example, line through (1,1),(2,2) is y=x (2 points); line through (1,2),(2,1) is slope -1 (2 points); line through (1,3),(2,2) is slope -1? For n=4, can we cover 10 points with 3 lines? Possibly if we use a line through many points. For example, line y=x passes through (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) but (3,3) not in set for n=4 because a+b=6>5? So three lines can cover at most 6 points if each covers 2, but we need 10. So minimum is 4. So the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points is exactly n. So for n lines, it's tight: we cannot do with fewer, and we can do with exactly n (using vertical lines, or horizontal, or slope -1). So the configuration is essentially "critical". We need to use exactly n lines, and we have the freedom to choose any lines (not necessarily covering many points). The condition is that the lines must be distinct and cover all points. Since we need exactly n lines, it's a critical covering. So the lines must be "in general position" in some sense, but they can have overlaps.

So correct line: y = -1/2 x + 4. Good. So that line passes through (4,2) and (6,1). So that's sunny. Good.

Now check (4,3) and (5,1): slope (1-3)/(5-4) = -2, line: y-3 = -2(x-4) => y = -2x + 11. At x=5, y = -10+11=1. So passes through (5,1). So that's sunny.

Let's think more generally. The condition is that we need to cover a triangular grid with n lines. Since the minimal number of lines is n, the configuration is essentially a "critical" covering. There is a known concept: "covering the integer points in a triangle with lines" is related to "Sylvester's problem" and "Gallai's theorem". Check coverage: (3,1) on L1; (4,2) on L1? at x=4 gives y=2, yes; (3,2) on L2; (4,1) on L3? at x=4 gives y=-8+9=1, yes; (5,1) on L3? at x=5 gives y=-10+9=-1, no; (5,1) not on L3. (5,1) is on L2? at x=5 gives y=-2.5+3.5=1, yes L2: y=-1/2 x+7/2 gives at x=5: -2.5+3.5=1. So (5,1) on L2. Also (4,2) on L1; (3,3) on L3; (4,1) on L3; (3,2) on L2; (5,1) on L2; (4,2) on L1; (3,1) on L1. So all points covered. So this works. So for n=5, we can take non-sunny lines: x=1,2 (2 lines) and then sunny lines: L1: y=x-2, L2: y=-1/2 x+7/2, L3: y=-2x+9. That's 5 lines total. So k=3 works for n=5 as well. For n=4, the points with a=2,3,4: (2,1-2),(3,1-2),(4,1). That's 2+2+1=5 points? Actually for n=4, the triangle points are: (1,1-4),(2,1-3),(3,1-2),(4,1). If we take x=1, then remaining points: (2,1-3),(3,1-2),(4,1) -> 3+2+1=6 points. So n-3=1 vertical line x=1, leaving 6 points. That's the triangle of side 3. We need 3 sunny lines covering those 6 points. For n=4, the points are: (2,1-3),(3,1-2),(4,1). Can we cover with 3 sunny lines? Try similar: (2,1)-(3,2) slope1: L1: y=x-1? At x=2 gives y=1; at x=3 gives y=2; (2,2)-(4,1) slope (1-2)/(4-2) = -1/2, L2: y-2=-1/2(x-2) => y=-1/2 x+3; (2,3)-(3,1) slope (1-3)/(3-2)=-2, L3: y-3=-2(x-2) => y=-2x+7. For n=3, we have all sunny lines covering 6 points; that's k=3. So indeed, for all n≥3, we can take n-3 vertical lines x=1,...,n-3 and then 3 sunny lines covering the remaining 6 points. So k=3 is always possible.

Now what about k=2? Is it possible for any n? We need to find a configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines. That would mean n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover many points; the complement must be covered by 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from the complement (if no triple), the complement size must be at most 4. Also, if the complement has a triple on a sunny line, then one sunny line covers 3 points, and the other covers the remaining point(s). So complement size could be 3 or 4. In general, we need to cover the complement with 2 sunny lines. The complement is the set of points not on any of the n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are of types: horizontal, vertical, slope -1. We need to choose n-2 such lines that together cover most points, leaving at most 4 points. The maximum union from n-2 non-sunny lines is? Each non-sunny line can cover at most n points (if it's x=1 or y=1 or x+y=n+1). But we have many lines; we can take the largest ones. For n≥3, the sum of sizes of the n-2 lines with the largest sizes is: the lines x=1 (n points), x=2 (n-1), x=3 (n-2), ..., x=n-2 (3 points). Sum = n + (n-1) + ... + 3 = (n(n+1)/2) - (1+2) = n(n+1)/2 - 3. That's the total number of points in the triangle minus 3? Actually total points = n(n+1)/2. So sum = total - 3. So the maximum union from n-2 lines (if they are pairwise disjoint) is n(n+1)/2 - 3. That would leave complement of size 3. So if we take the n-2 vertical lines x=1,...,n-2, they are disjoint and cover n + (n-1) + ... + 3 = total - 3 points. The complement is the triangle of side 3: points with a = n-1, n, and possibly n+1? Actually for n, the triangle points: a from 1 to n; for a=n, only (n,1). For a=n-1, b=1..2; for a=n-2, b=1..3; etc. So after taking x=1..n-2, the remaining points are: a=n-1: b=1,2; a=n: b=1. That's 3 points. So complement size=3. Now we need to cover these 3 points with 2 sunny lines. As before, this is only possible if those 3 points are collinear on a sunny line. But the 3 points are (n-1,1), (n-1,2), (n,1). Are they on a sunny line? Check slope between (n-1,1) and (n-1,2) is infinite; between (n-1,1) and (n,1) is 0; between (n-1,2) and (n,1) is -1. So not collinear. So that fails. Could we choose a different set of n-2 non-sunny lines that leaves a complement of 3 points that are on a sunny line? Possibly by using a mix of horizontal and vertical lines. For instance, take x=1, y=1, and then other lines that are disjoint? The maximum union from n-2 lines might be less than total-3 if we use lines that overlap. To get complement size 3, we need union= total-3. That is the maximum possible union from n-2 lines if we take the n-2 lines with the largest sizes that are disjoint. The only disjoint sets of lines that achieve total-3 are the vertical lines x=1..n-2 or the horizontal lines y=1..n-2, or possibly a combination that covers the same number? For n=4, total-3=7, union from 2 lines: x=1 and x=2 give 4+3=7; y=1 and y=2 give 4+3=7; also x=1 and y=1 give 4+4-1=7; so there are multiple sets. But in all cases, the complement is a set of 3 points that are not on a sunny line. For n=4, complement after x=1 and x=2 is (3,1),(3,2),(4,1) — not collinear. After y=1 and y=2 is (1,3),(2,3),(4,1)? Thus for n=4, k=2 impossible. For n=5, complement after x=1..3 is 3 points: (4,1),(4,2),(5,1) — not collinear. Therefore k=2 is impossible for all n≥3.

Now what about k=0,1,3? We have k=0 possible (all vertical lines). k=1 possible (n-1 vertical lines + one sunny line through (n,1)). k=3 possible (n-3 vertical lines + 3 sunny lines covering the remaining 6 points). Are there any other k? For n=3, k=3 is possible, but also maybe k=2? No. So for n=3, possible k: 0,1,3. For n=4,5,6,... we have k=0,1,3 possible. But could k be larger than 3? For n=4, k=4 impossible because total lines=4, and each sunny line covers at most 2 points, total points=10, need 4 lines, if all sunny, max coverage 8<10. So k=4 impossible. For n=5, k=4? 4 sunny lines would need to cover 15 points; each sunny line max 3, total max 12<15, so impossible. For n=6, k=4? 4 sunny lines max 12, total points=21, need to cover 21 with 6 lines, if 4 sunny, 2 non-sunny, max coverage = 4*3 + 2*n? Actually non-sunny can cover up to n points each. For n=6, if we take 2 non-sunny lines, max union=6+5=11 (if disjoint), complement=10; 4 sunny lines max 12, total max 22, but we need to cover 21 points; it's possible in principle if the non-sunny lines cover 11 and sunny lines cover 10, but we need exactly 4 sunny lines. Could we have a configuration with 4 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines that covers all 21 points? For n=6, total=21. The maximum union from 2 non-sunny lines is 11 (x=1,x=2) or 11 (x=1,y=1) etc. So complement=10. Then we need 4 sunny lines to cover those 10 points. Since each sunny line can cover at most 3, 4 lines can cover at most 12. So possible. But we need to find such a set. For n=6, we already have a construction with 3 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines (x=1,2,3). Could we have 4 sunny lines? For instance, take non-sunny lines: x=1 and x=2 (2 lines covering 6+5=11 points). Complement = 10 points: a=3..6: (3,1-4); (4,1-3); (5,1-2); (6,1). That's 4+3+2+1=10 points. We need to cover these 10 points with 4 sunny lines. Is that possible? We need to partition these 10 points into 4 lines, each sunny, with at most 3 points per line. The points are the triangle for n=4? Actually points with a≥3 for n=6 is the triangle for n=4? For n=4, total=10; the points are: (1,1-4),(2,1-3),(3,1-2),(4,1). Thus the only possible k are 0,1,3.

Now we should also consider if k could be any number for n=3? Already done. For n=4, we have 0,1,3. Could k=2? We argued no. So answer: k ∈ {0,1,3} for any n ≥ 3.

Thus the final answer: The set of all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying the conditions is exactly {0,1,3} (i.e., k = 0, 1, or 3). Note that n is given and n ≥ 3, so these are the only possibilities.

We are given: "A line in the plane is called sunny if it is not parallel to any of the x-axis, the y-axis, or the line x+y=0." So a line is sunny if its slope is not 0, not infinite (i.e., not vertical), and not -1. That is, a line is sunny if its slope is defined and not 0, not -1. Also note that lines parallel to x-axis have slope 0; parallel to y-axis are vertical (infinite slope); parallel to x+y=0 has slope -1. So sunny lines are those with slope m ≠ 0, ∞, -1. So they are lines that are not horizontal, not vertical, and not with slope -1.

x=1 and x=4: union = 4+1=5, complement size 5; too large for 2 sunny lines (since each sunny line max 2 points, total 4). So not possible.

Thus for n=4, k=2 seems impossible. But we need to be thorough: Could there be a choice of two non-sunny lines that are not both of the types that cover many points, but maybe one non-sunny line covers only 1 point, leaving a large complement, but then we have 2 sunny lines that can cover up to 4 points; if the complement size is ≤4, we might still cover it with 2 sunny lines. So we need to consider all possible pairs of non-sunny lines, not just those that cover many points. The condition for k=2 is that we have 2 sunny lines and 2 non-sunny lines (total 4 lines). The non-sunny lines can be any lines that are not sunny (horizontal, vertical, slope -1). They could be lines that cover very few points (e.g., y=4 covers only (1,4), x=4 covers only (4,1), x+y=2 covers only (1,1), etc.). If we choose such lines, the union may be small, leaving a large complement (≥5 points), which cannot be covered by 2 sunny lines (since each sunny line covers at most 2 points from the set, total 4). So the complement must be ≤4. So we need the union of the two non-sunny lines to cover at least 6 points. So we only need to consider pairs that cover at least 6 points. For n=4, the maximum union from two lines is 8 (if they are disjoint), but we need at least 6. So we need to consider all pairs that cover at least 6 points. Let's enumerate all possible pairs (L1, L2) where L1 and L2 are non-sunny lines (horizontal, vertical, slope -1) and compute their union size and the complement. Since the set is small, we can brute force mentally. Let's list all non-sunny lines:

Horizontal: H1: y=1 (points: (1,1),(2,1),(3,1),(4,1)); H2: y=2 (points: (1,2),(2,2),(3,2)); H3: y=3 (points: (1,3),(2,3)); H4: y=4 (points: (1,4)).
Vertical: V1: x=1 (points: (1,1),(1,2),(1,3),(1,4)); V2: x=2 (points: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3)); V3: x=3 (points: (3,1),(3,2)); V4: x=4 (points: (4,1)).
Slope -1: D2: x+y=2 (points: (1,1)); D3: x+y=3 (points: (1,2),(2,1)); D4: x+y=4 (points: (1,3),(2,2),(3,1)); D5: x+y=5 (points: (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)).

Now, compute union sizes for each pair. We need union size ≥6. Since total points =10, complement size ≤4. Let's compute all pairs:

- V1 (4) and V2 (3): disjoint? V1 points: (1,1-4); V2 points: (2,1-3). They are disjoint because x=1 vs x=2. Union size = 4+3=7. Complement =3 points: (3,1),(3,2),(4,1). Already considered: no sunny pair.

- V1 and V3: V1 4, V3 2, disjoint? V1 x=1, V3 x=3, disjoint. Union=6, complement=4: (2,1),(2,2),(2,3),(4,1). But could we cover the four points with two sunny lines where one line covers three points? No three collinear sunny. So we need to check if there is a covering of these four points by two sunny lines, not necessarily each line covering exactly two points from the set. For instance, maybe one sunny line covers (2,2) and (4,1) (sunny) and the other covers (2,1) and (2,3) but that line is vertical not sunny; but could the second line cover (2,1) alone and (2,3) alone? No, one line can only cover one of them if we cannot have a line through both that is sunny. So we need to cover all four points with two lines; each point must be on at least one line. So if we have a line L1 that goes through two points, the remaining points must be covered by L2. If L2 covers two points, it must go through them; but if the only possible pair from the remaining two is not sunny, then L2 cannot be sunny. So we need to see if there is a partition of the set into two pairs, each pair forming a sunny line. The only sunny pair among the four is (2,2)-(4,1). The remaining two are (2,1) and (2,3). The line through them is vertical, not sunny. So that partition fails. Could we have a different partition where the two pairs are not disjoint? For covering, we can have L1 cover one point, L2 cover the other three, but then L2 must cover three points; can it cover (2,1),(2,2),(2,3) with a sunny line? Actually if L2 is a line, it can contain at most 2 of these points if they are collinear; but if it contains only one, then the other two points would need to be covered by L1? But we have only two lines. So we need to cover all points. Let's denote the four points: A=(2,1), B=(2,2), C=(2,3), D=(4,1). We need two lines L1, L2 such that each of A,B,C,D lies on at least one of them. Since L1 and L2 are lines, they are infinite. The points are not collinear in triples; check if any three are collinear? A,B,C are collinear vertical x=2; B,C,D: B(2,2), C(2,3), D(4,1) are not collinear (slope from B to D = (1-2)/(4-2) = -1/2, from C to D = (1-3)/(4-2) = -2/2 = -1, not equal). So no three collinear. So each line can contain at most 2 points from the set. Thus we need to partition the four points into two pairs, each pair lying on a line (the line through the pair). So the only possible covering with two lines is that L1 is the line through a pair, L2 is the line through the other pair. So we need to find a partition of the set into two pairs such that both pairs are collinear (they always are) and the lines are sunny. So we need to check all possible pairings of the four points. The possible pairings: (A,B) and (C,D): lines: x=2 (vertical) and line through (2,3) and (4,1): slope -1, not sunny. (A,C) and (B,D): lines: x=2 (vertical) and line through (2,2) and (4,1): slope -1/2, sunny. So (A,C) gives vertical (non-sunny), (B,D) gives sunny. So if we pair (A,C) and (B,D), then L1 is vertical (non-sunny) and L2 sunny; that's not allowed because we need both lines sunny. So that pairing fails. (A,D) and (B,C): lines: through (2,1) and (4,1) is horizontal y=1 (non-sunny), and through (2,2) and (2,3) is vertical x=2 (non-sunny). So both non-sunny. Thus V1 and V3 fails.

Thus for n=4, k=2 seems impossible. So far we have k=0,1,3 possible. What about k=2 for n=5? Let's test a potential construction. For n=5, points: (1,1)-(1,5); (2,1)-(2,4); (3,1)-(3,3); (4,1)-(4,2); (5,1). That's 15 points. We need 5 lines, exactly k sunny. For k=2, we need 2 sunny lines and 3 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover many points; we need to cover all 15 points with 5 lines. The maximum coverage by a non-sunny line: x=1 covers 5 points; y=1 covers 5; x+y=6 covers 5 points (since a+b=6: (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1)). So each non-sunny line can cover at most 5 points. With 3 non-sunny lines, we could cover up to 15 points if they are disjoint. But can we choose 3 non-sunny lines that together cover all 15 points? We need to find a general characterization. Let's think about the necessary condition. The condition that for all positive integers a,b with a+b ≤ n+1, point (a,b) lies on at least one line. This is a covering problem. We have n lines. The points are a triangular grid. The lines can be any lines, not necessarily through two points. The restriction on sunny lines is that they cannot be horizontal, vertical, or slope -1. So they can have any other slope.

Observation: The set of points is symmetric under permutations of coordinates? Not exactly, but the condition a+b ≤ n+1 is symmetric in a and b? Actually if we swap a and b, the set is the same because a+b ≤ n+1 and a,b positive. So the set is symmetric under swapping coordinates. The lines we use are also symmetric in some sense.

Another approach: Consider the integer lattice points. The lines are infinite. The condition that every point in the triangle lies on some line. This is reminiscent of covering a triangle with lines, which is a classic problem. For the set of points (i,j) with i,j positive and i+j ≤ n+1, the minimum number of lines needed to cover them is n (since we can use n vertical lines, or n horizontal lines, or n lines of slope -1). So for n lines, it's tight: we cannot do with fewer, and we can do with exactly n (using vertical lines, or horizontal, or slope -1). So the configuration is essentially "critical". We need to find if any such set works.

We already have a candidate: non-sunny lines: x=1,2,3,4,y=3 gives union=28, complement=8 points: (5,1),(5,2),(5,4),(6,1),(6,2),(7,1),(7,2),(8,1). We need to cover these 8 points with 3 sunny lines. Each sunny line can cover at most 3 points from this set. So possible if we can find 3 lines that cover all 8 points. Let's attempt to find a covering. The points are arranged in a triangular pattern. Thus for n=8, only k=0,1 seem possible. For n=9, maybe k=3 possible? Let's test the pattern. It seems that the possible k are 0,1, and maybe 3 for small n, but for larger n, k=3 becomes impossible. The problem asks: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying both conditions." So the answer may be: k can be any integer from 0 to n except 2? But we saw for n=3,4,5,6, k=3 works; for n=7, k=3 fails; so the condition might be: k can be 0,1, and n-2? For n=5, n-2=3 works; for n=6, n-2=4 fails; for n=7, n-2=5 fails; so not.

Maybe we need to find a general necessary condition based on the number of points on the lines. The condition that we have n lines covering all points. Since the minimal number is n, the covering must be "tight". There is a known result: For the triangular grid of points (i,j) with i+j ≤ n+1, the only way to cover all points with n lines is to take all lines of one type (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). But we have counterexamples for n=4,5,6 where we used 1 vertical + 3 sunny, or 2 non-sunny + 3 sunny, etc. So that's not true. However, perhaps the condition is that the number of sunny lines is at most 3? And for n≥7, the maximum number of sunny lines is 1? Let's test n=6, we had k=3 possible. For n=5, k=3 possible. Now what about k=2? Is it possible for any n? We need to find a configuration with exactly 2 sunny lines. That would mean n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines can cover many points; the complement must be covered by 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from the complement (if no triple), the complement size must be at most 4. Also, if the complement has a triple on a sunny line, then one sunny line covers 3 points, and the other covers the remaining point(s). So complement size could be 3 or 4. In general, we need to cover the complement with 2 sunny lines. The complement is the set of points not on any of the n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are of types: horizontal, vertical, slope -1. We need to choose n-2 such lines that together cover most points, leaving at most 4 points. The maximum union from n-2 non-sunny lines is? Each non-sunny line can cover at most n points (if it's x=1 or y=1 or x+y=n+1). But we have many lines; we can take the largest ones. For n≥3, the sum of sizes of the n-2 lines with the largest sizes is: the lines x=1 (n points), x=2 (n-1), x=3 (n-2), ..., x=n-2 (3 points). Sum = n + (n-1) + ... + 3 = (n(n+1)/2) - (1+2) = n(n+1)/2 - 3. That's the total number of points in the triangle minus 3? Actually total points = n(n+1)/2. So sum = total - 3. So the maximum union from n-2 lines (if they are pairwise disjoint) is n(n+1)/2 - 3. That would leave complement of size 3. So if we take the n-2 vertical lines x=1,...,n-2, they are disjoint and cover n + (n-1) + ... + 3 = total - 3 points. The complement is the triangle of side 3: points with a = n-1, n, and possibly n+1? Actually for n, the triangle points: a from 1 to n; for a=n, only (n,1). For a=n-1, b=1..2; for a=n-2, b=1..3; etc. So after taking x=1..n-2, the remaining points are: a=n-1: b=1,2; a=n: b=1. That's 3 points. So complement size=3. Now we need to cover these 3 points with 2 sunny lines. As before, this is only possible if those 3 points are collinear on a sunny line. But the 3 points are (n-1,1), (n-1,2), (n,1). Are they on a sunny line? Check slope between (n-1,1) and (n-1,2) is infinite; between (n-1,1) and (n,1) is 0; between (n-1,2) and (n,1) is -1. So not collinear. So that fails. Could we choose a different set of n-2 non-sunny lines that leaves a complement of 3 points that are on a sunny line? Possibly by using a mix of horizontal and vertical lines. For instance, take x=1, y=1, and then other lines that are disjoint? The maximum union from n-2 lines might be less than total-3 if we use lines that overlap. To get complement size 3, we need union= total-3. That is the maximum possible union from n-2 lines if we take the n-2 lines with the largest sizes that are disjoint. The only disjoint sets of lines that achieve total-3 are the vertical lines x=1..n-2 or the horizontal lines y=1..n-2, or possibly a combination that covers the same number? For n=4, total-3=7, union from 2 lines: x=1 and x=2 give 4+3=7; y=1 and y=2 give 4+3=7; also x=1 and y=1 give 4+4-1=7; so there are multiple sets. But in all cases, the complement is a set of 3 points that are not on a sunny line. For n=4, complement after x=1 and x=2 is (3,1),(3,2),(4,1) — not collinear. After y=1 and y=2 is (1,3),(2,3),(4,1)? Thus for n=4, k=2 impossible. For n=5, complement after x=1..3 is 3 points: (4,1),(4,2),(5,1) — not collinear. What about using lines of slope -1? For n=5, take x+y=6 (points (1,5),(2,4),(3,3),(4,2),(5,1)) and x+y=5 (points (1,4),(2,3),(3,2),(4,1)) and x+y=4? Thus it seems that for any n≥3, if we try to use n-2 non-sunny lines to cover all but 3 points, the complement will be a set of 3 points that are not on a sunny line. Could we have a complement of size 4 covered by 2 sunny lines? That would require that the union of n-2 non-sunny lines is total-4. The maximum union from n-2 lines is total-3 (as we saw), so we can achieve total-4 only if we have some overlap that reduces the union by 1. For example, if we take x=1..n-2 but replace one line with a line that covers one fewer point, the union might become total-4. Then complement size=4. Can we cover 4 points with 2 sunny lines? What about using other lines? Could we achieve complement size 4 with a different set? For n=4, we need 2 non-sunny lines covering 6 points. Thus k=2 fails for n=4.

Thus the final answer: All nonnegative integers k such that k ∈ {0,1,3} for any n ≥ 3.

But wait, the problem statement: "Determine all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines in the plane satisfying both conditions." So for a given n, we need to list k. Since n is arbitrary but fixed, the answer might be: k can be 0, 1, or 3, and these are the only possibilities for any n ≥ 3. However, is k=3 always possible for n=3? Yes, we have a construction with 3 sunny lines covering all 6 points. For n=3, we also have k=0,1. So the set of possible k is {0,1,3} for n=3, and {0,1,3} for n≥4 as well. So lines: L1: through (1,1) and (2,2) -> y=x; L2: through (1,2) and (3,1) -> slope -1/2, equation: y-2 = -1/2(x-1) => y = -1/2 x + 2.5; L3: through (1,3) and (2,1) -> slope (1-3)/(2-1) = -2, equation: y-3 = -2(x-1) => y = -2x+5. Thus k=3 is possible for all n≥3.

Now what about k=2? We argued impossible for all n≥3. But we should also consider n=3,4,5,6,7,8... and we have a systematic argument: For k=2, we would need n-2 non-sunny lines covering all but at most 4 points, and 2 sunny lines covering those points. The maximum number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is 4 (each at most 2, if no triple). However, if the complement has a triple, then one sunny line can cover 3, and the other covers the remaining point(s), so complement size could be 3 or 4. So we need the union of n-2 non-sunny lines to be total - 3 or total - 4. The maximum union from n-2 lines is total - 3 (by taking x=1..n-2). So the only way to get complement size 3 is to achieve union = total-3. For complement size 3, we need 2 sunny lines covering 3 points. That is possible only if those 3 points are collinear on a sunny line. But the only set of 3 points that are on a sunny line from the complement of x=1..n-2 are (n-1,1), (n-1,2), (n,1) which are not collinear. Could there be a different set of n-2 non-sunny lines that achieve union = total-3 but leaves a different complement of 3 points that are collinear? Possibly by using a mix of horizontal and vertical lines, we could get a different complement. For example, take x=1 and y=1? For n=5, x=1 and y=1 give union=5+5-1=9, complement=6 points, not 3. To get complement=3, we need union=12 (since total=15). x=1 and y=2? x1:5, y2:4, overlap (1,2) gives union=5+4-1=8, complement=7. Thus the only possible k are 0,1,3.

Now we should also consider if k could be any number for n=3? Already done. For n=4, we have 0,1,3. Could k=2? We argued no. So answer: k ∈ {0,1,3} for any n ≥ 3.

Thus the final answer: The set of all nonnegative integers k such that there exist n distinct lines satisfying the conditions is exactly {0,1,3} (i.e., k = 0, 1, or 3). Note that n is given and n ≥ 3, so these are the only possibilities.

The solution should include:

- Proof that k=2 is impossible (and any k≥4 impossible). The argument: For k≥4, the total points covered by the sunny lines would be at most 3k (since each sunny line can cover at most 3 points from the triangle? For a line with slope 1, the points are of the form (a, a+c) for some c. Then condition a+(a+c) ≤ n+1 => 2a+c ≤ n+1 => a ≤ (n+1-c)/2. So the maximum number of points on such a line is floor((n+1-c)/2). For c=0, max points = floor(n/2). For c=1, max = floor((n)/2). For c=2, floor((n-1)/2). So the maximum number of points a sunny line can contain is about n/2, not 3. So earlier claim that each sunny line covers at most 3 points is not true for larger n. For n=6, we had a sunny line with 3 points (y=x). For n=7, the line y=x gives points (1,1),(2,2),(3,3) — 3 points. For n=8, y=x gives (1,1),(2,2),(3,3),(4,4) — 4 points? So our earlier argument that each sunny line covers at most 2 points from the complement for n=6 was correct because the complement had only 6 points, and no sunny line had more than 2 of them. Now for k≥4, we need to argue that it's impossible. The total number of points is N = n(n+1)/2. If we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most some number of points, but the non-sunny lines also cover points. We need to show that for any configuration with exactly k sunny lines, the total number of points covered by the lines must be at least N. But we can use a counting argument based on the fact that the only lines that can cover many points are the vertical, horizontal, and slope -1 lines. But we have a mix. The argument for k≥4 might be that the sum of the sizes of the lines (the number of points from the triangle on each line) is at most something, and the overlaps are limited. But we need a clean necessary condition.

Alternatively, we can use the following approach: Consider the set of points P. The lines are distinct. For each point in P, it lies on at least one line. The number of points that can be covered by a single line is at most n (if it's x=1 or y=1 or x+y=n+1). So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most kn points? But that's too large; we need a tighter bound.

Maybe we can use a combinatorial geometry lemma: For the triangular grid of points, the minimum number of lines needed to cover all points is n. Moreover, any covering with n lines must consist of exactly n-1 lines of one type and one line of another type? But we have counterexamples: For n=4, we covered with 1 vertical and 3 sunny lines. That's 4 lines, but the lines are not all of the same type. However, note that the sunny lines we used are not of the type that cover many points; they cover only 2 points each. So the total coverage by these 3 sunny lines is 6 points, but they cover 6 points, while the vertical line covers 4 points, total 10 points. So the sum of points on the lines (with multiplicity) is 4+2+2+2=10, which equals the total points. So the covering is "exact" in the sense that the lines are disjoint in the points they cover? In our construction, the vertical line x=1 covers 4 points; the sunny lines cover 2 each, but they are disjoint from the vertical line? The vertical line covers (1,1-4); the sunny lines cover (2,1),(3,2),(2,2),(4,1),(2,3),(3,1) — these are all distinct from (1,1-4) because a≠1. So yes, the lines are pairwise disjoint in the points they cover. So the union of the lines is exactly the set of points. So in general, any covering with n lines must have the property that the lines are pairwise disjoint (no point lies on two lines) because if a point lies on two lines, then the sum of points on lines would exceed the total points? Actually it's possible that some points lie on two lines, but then the total sum of points on lines would be greater than N, but we have only n lines, each covering at most n points, so sum ≤ n^2, which is not a contradiction. However, in our constructions for k=0,1,3, we have the property that the lines are pairwise disjoint in the points they cover. For k=0 (all vertical), they are disjoint because different x. For k=1, vertical lines are disjoint, and the sunny line covers only one point, which is not on any vertical line because a=n, not ≤n-1. So disjoint. For k=3, the vertical lines are disjoint, and the three sunny lines cover the remaining 6 points, which are not on the vertical lines. So disjoint.

Maybe it's a necessary condition that any covering of the triangular grid with n lines must be a partition of the points into n lines, each line covering a set of points that are collinear, and the lines are pairwise disjoint. This is plausible because if two lines share a point, then the total number of points on the lines would be less than the sum of their sizes, but the sum of sizes could still be N if overlaps are minimal. However, we can try to prove that any covering with exactly n lines must be a partition (i.e., the lines are disjoint). Suppose two lines L and M share a point P. Then the union of the lines covers all points, but the total number of points covered by the lines (counting each point once) is N. The sum of the sizes of the lines is |L|+|M|+... . Since L and M share at least one point, the sum of sizes is at least N+1. But each line can cover at most n points (for vertical, horizontal, slope -1). The sum of sizes is ≤ n * n = n^2. That's not a contradiction. However, we can use the fact that the maximum size of a line is n, and the number of lines is n, so the sum of sizes could be as high as n^2, but we need the union to be exactly N, so the overlaps must be such that the sum of sizes = N + (overlaps). So it's possible. For example, if we take two vertical lines x=1 and x=2, they cover 4+3=7 points, but total points for n=4 is 10, so we need more lines. The overlaps could be minimal. So it's not necessary that the lines are disjoint.

But for our purpose, we need to show that k cannot be 2. We already have a plausible argument based on the complement size. Let's try to give a rigorous proof that k=2 is impossible. We'll use a counting argument: Suppose we have exactly 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines. The non-sunny lines are of three types: horizontal (slope 0), vertical (infinite slope), or slope -1. Each such line can cover at most n points from the triangle (if it's x=1 or y=1 or x+y=n+1). However, the total number of points in the triangle is N = n(n+1)/2. The union of the n-2 non-sunny lines covers some points; the remaining points must be covered by the 2 sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most 2 points from the complement (because if a sunny line contained 3 points from the complement, then those 3 points would be collinear on a sunny line, but the complement is a set of points that we can analyze). However, we need a more general argument: For any set of points that is the complement of some union of n-2 lines, the maximum number of points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines is at most 4 (each at most 2). But we need to show that the complement size is at least 5, leading to contradiction. For k=2, we need the complement size to be at most 4. So we need to show that the union of n-2 non-sunny lines cannot cover all but 3 or 4 points. The maximum possible union size from n-2 non-sunny lines is N-3 (by taking x=1..n-2). So the complement size is at least 3. To have complement size ≤4, we need the union to be at least N-4. Is it possible to achieve union size N-4? Possibly by taking x=1..n-2 but replacing one line with a line that covers one fewer point, or by using a different combination. For example, take x=1..n-3 and y=1? That would cover (n-3) + (n-2) + ... + 3 + 5? Not sure. We need to examine the maximum possible union size from n-2 lines. The maximum is N-3, as we said. The next possible union size might be N-4. So we need to check if there is a set of n-2 non-sunny lines whose union is N-4. That would leave 4 points. Can we cover those 4 points with 2 sunny lines? Possibly if the 4 points can be partitioned into two sunny pairs. So we need to examine if such a configuration exists for some n.

We can try to construct for n=4: N=10, N-4=6. We need 2 non-sunny lines covering 6 points. As we saw, x=2 and y=1 cover 6 points. The complement is 4 points: (1,2),(1,3),(2,1),(3,1)? Thus it seems that for n=5, any set of 3 lines covering 11 points leaves a complement of 4 points that is not coverable by 2 sunny lines. Possibly for all n, the complement after n-2 lines is a triangle of side 3 (if we take vertical lines x=1..n-2) or a set of points that does not allow covering by 2 sunny lines. We need a general proof that k=2 is impossible.

Maybe we can use a parity or combinatorial argument based on the fact that the set of points has some structure, and the only way to cover it with n lines is to use lines of three types, and the number of sunny lines is at most 3? But we need to prove that k cannot be 2.

Another approach: Consider the lattice points. For each line, define its "type" as the slope. The condition that every point lies on at least one line. We can use a double counting of incidences: Count the number of pairs (point, line) such that the point lies on the line. This is at least N. On the other hand, each line can contain at most n points, so total incidences ≤ n * n = n^2. That's not helpful.

But we can use a more refined bound: The maximum number of points on a line of slope 0 is n, on slope ∞ is n, on slope -1 is n. For other slopes, the maximum number is at most floor(n/2). So if we have k sunny lines, they can cover at most k * floor(n/2) points. The non-sunny lines cover at most n * (n-2) points. So total incidences ≤ n(n-2) + k floor(n/2). But we need incidences ≥ N. For n large, n(n-2) is about n^2, which is much larger than N = n^2/2. So that doesn't give a contradiction.

Perhaps we can use a combinatorial geometry lemma: For the triangular grid, the only way to cover all points with n lines is to take all lines of one type (horizontal, vertical, or slope -1). But we have counterexamples: For n=4, we covered with 1 vertical and 3 sunny lines. However, note that the sunny lines we used are not of the type that cover many points; they cover only 2 each. So the covering is essentially a partition. For n=4, the vertical line covers 4 points, the sunny lines cover 2 each, total 10 points. So it's a partition. In general, any covering with exactly n lines must be a partition? Not necessarily, but maybe it must be a partition because if two lines intersect at a point, then the total number of points on the lines would be less than the sum of their sizes, and since the sum of sizes is at most n^2, we could still have N. However, we can try to prove that for any covering with n lines, the sum of the sizes of the lines is at most N + (n-1)? That doesn't make sense.

Let's try to prove that k=2 is impossible by contradiction. Suppose we have 2 sunny lines and n-2 non-sunny lines covering all points. The non-sunny lines are of types H, V, D. Each such line can cover at most n points. But we can use a counting argument based on the fact that the sunny lines have slope not 0, ∞, -1. Consider the set of points that lie on the sunny lines. Since each sunny line can cover at most floor(n/2) points (as we argued, for a line with slope not 0,∞,-1, the maximum number of lattice points within the triangle is at most floor(n/2)), but we can bound it by n/2. That's not a strong bound.

Alternatively, consider the following: The points P are symmetric under swapping coordinates. The only lines that can contain many points are those of slope 0, ∞, -1. So if we have 2 sunny lines, they can contain at most 2 * floor(n/2) points. The non-sunny lines can contain at most n*(n-2) points, but they may overlap. The total union of all lines is N. So we have N ≤ (n*(n-2) + 2*floor(n/2)). For n≥3, this is false? Let's test n=4: n*(n-2)=4*2=8; 2*floor(4/2)=2*2=4; sum=12; N=10, so 10 ≤ 12 true. n=5: 5*3=15; 2*floor(5/2)=2*2=4; sum=19; N=15, true. n=6: 6*4=24; 2*3=6; sum=30; N=21, true. So not a contradiction.

But we can also use a lower bound on the number of points on the non-sunny lines: They must cover at least N - 2*floor(n/2) points. Since the maximum union from n-2 lines is N-3 (as we saw), we need N - 2*floor(n/2) ≤ N-3 => 2*floor(n/2) ≥ 3, which holds for n≥3. So that doesn't help.

Maybe we can use the following fact: In any covering with n lines, the lines must be "critical" in the sense that they cannot be replaced by other lines without breaking coverage. There might be a known result: The only critical coverings of the integer points in a triangle are those that consist of all lines of one type. But our examples contradict that.

Now if we want union N-4, we need to reduce the union by 1 from that maximum. But is it possible that for some n, the complement of a set of n-2 non-sunny lines (which might be a mix) is a set of 4 points that can be covered by 2 sunny lines? We need to check all possibilities. Since the problem is from an olympiad, likely the answer is that k can only be 0,1,3. We can try to prove that any covering with n lines must have the property that the number of sunny lines is at most 3, and if it is exactly 2, then there is a contradiction. We can also try to prove that k cannot be 2 by using a parity argument on the x-coordinates or using the fact that the sum of the x-coordinates of the points on the sunny lines is something.
